Defuturing

## Page 3

RADICAL THINKERS IN DESIGN

             Series Editors: Clive Dilnot and Eduardo Staszowski

Expansion in practice and the global increase in the numbers of those with
design education has not brought with it increased understanding. On the
contrary, despite the intelligence of many of those entering the field, reduced
to the crudest understanding of vocation, depth of thought disappears, crises
remain untouched, genuinely new practices and conceptions struggle to be
comprehended in their implications.

Radical Thinkers in Design, a moment of the larger project Designing in
Dark Times, seeks in a small way to try to address this situation by translating
or bringing back into circulation, as aids to thinking and praxis, some key
provocative texts in contemporary thinking on designing.

Forthcoming Titles in the Series

          John Chris Jones, designing designing (1984)
      Tony Fry, Defuturing: A New Design Philosophy (1999)
               Judy Attfield, Wild Things (2000)
        Anthony Dunne and Fiona Raby, Design Noir (2001)

As acting in the world descends ever deeper towards negation, these books
offer counter-perspectives. In what they open towards, what they explore
and present, and above all in what they anticipate, they point to the concrete
possibilities, as well as to the necessity, of paradigm shifts in design thinking
and in our conceptions of what designing today can and should be.
   Offering approaches, concepts, modes of thinking and models of practice
that show how designing can not only can be re-thought and re-positioned in
its internal momentum but also that it offers, at least potentially, an integral
mode and capacity of acting in the world. By showing how, at base, designing
contains irreplaceable critical, constitutive and critically affirmative moments,
they point us towards ways of reversing some of the negative and destructive
tendencies threatening to engulf the world.

A New Design Philosophy

                                                                                         ix

## Page 11

Tony Fry’s Defuturing:ANewDesign
Philosophy

Clive Dilnot

First published twenty years ago, but addressing concerns in design and in
the world that despite their urgency are even now scarcely beginning to be
adequately engaged, Tony Fry’s Defuturing: A New Design Philosophy is an
attempt to create what Fry calls ‘a new foundation of thought and practice’,
concerning design and the world, the basis of ‘another agenda of thinking,
making and living’ (p. xxix). That foundation and agenda has to do with how –
or in fact, whether – we are capable of thinking, with a view to acting well, in
regard to the potential for immanent catastrophe of what-is.
  On its first publication, an initial claim for Defuturing was that it offered an
archaeology of how the worlds we inhabit have been made unsustainable. As
readers discover, the premise is not inaccurate; its substantive chapters offer a
series of studies of just this. But to say this understates the force of the work,
the reasons why it has forced itself back into print. If the focus on history, rather
than abstraction reasoning, gives the work its concrete actuality, what compels
its reading today is conveyed better by the two moments of the book’s title:
   – ‘Defuturing’ as that which names the condition or trajectory of our times
and then becomes also the name of what critical thinking has to be in order
to be adequate to these times (‘for once one understands the nature and
magnitude of the defutured, how one accounts for the history of the material
world dramatically changes’ (p. 1);
   – ‘A New Design Philosophy’: the premise that the ‘seeing and making anew’
that arises from this attempt at understanding generates a new foundation of
design, which radically transforms what design is and does – but which also, in
the same moment, transforms ‘philosophy’, that which we take to be the truth
of things (p. 1).
   Secured by its third moment, history – which against the thrust of what
is so often said to be the case today proves to offer the grounds for future
praxis, and not least in designing – Defuturing is Fry’s early attempt at

x

## Page 12

TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿            xi

confronting both the ‘impossibility and the necessity’ of our condition and the
roles of designing, both in helping constitute this crisis but also offering, once
understood in this context, possible resources for its at least partial, if always
ultimately impossible, resolution.
   In this introduction, the aim is less to offer critical comments on Fry’s book
than to present the underlying structures of its key moments – while in effect
also implicitly asking the question as to how the present, including the present
of designing and its thinking, stands; how it means, in the face of this project.

                                         (1)

                          DEFUTURING

Defuturing is Fry’s name for the trajectory of our times. Its first meaning is
literal: ‘The negation of world futures for us, and many of our unknowing
non-human others’ (p. 10). Defuturing is then our condition. Specifically, it
is a condition of loss. It names the product of our extreme and never fully
acknowledged anthropocentrism, the condition where the self-centredness and
self-interest ‘of actions that have come to be a defining quality of our species’
have begun to turn back on themselves (p. 10). Defuturing is the loss, or at
least the erosion – to the point of immanent catastrophe – of the conjunctional
relations between things, ‘material and immaterial’, and the world; the loss
of the relations that often in all but silent ways, sustain and keep in place the
possibility of the conditions of the continuance of living things.
  The second meaning of Defuturing is as a paradox – if one that is scarcely
recognized in its fullest sense. The paradox is that this negation of world future
arrives for us as a product of how we have made, and ever more intensively
continue to make, the world, both materially and immaterially. More precisely,
what are in effect the autonomic processes of auto-destruction of the living
world are the outcomes of a making that has now become – but in truth
was never not – a taking-away. Defuturing names the condition where the
drive (the will) that everywhere ‘arranges itself in technology’, that is, in the
artificial, forces the ‘impossible on the possible’1 – and in ways that at once
exceed the capacities of natural systems to deal ‘as-is’ with the consequences
of this forcing (and which hence produces the disasters of climate change and
species extinction), but which also surpasses the necessarily limited ‘permitted’
capacities of social systems to manage and problem-solve in relation to these
second- and third-order consequences.2
  The scale of what is opened in this paradoxical taking-away makes what
is at stake here an entire model of civilization.3 In her book The Body in
Pain, Elaine Scarry offers at one point what seems, at first sight, to be an
extraordinary proposition: that ‘western religion and materialism suggest
that the on-going work of civilization is not simply making x or y but “making
making” itself, “remaking making”, rescuing, repairing, and restoring it to its
proper path each time it threatens to collapse into, or become conflated with

## Page 13

xii         TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿

its opposite’.4 The taking-away that Defuturing names is the reversal of this
project: civilization as the inversion of making into unmaking.5 Yet in a further
paradox of this situation, because it presents itself to us as without measure,
without end, so we fail even to describe it adequately. We lack the necessary
critical and interpretive categories through which to grasp it and to translate
into meaningful action what is involved here. Above all, we lack the ability to
see that what is also contained in this crisis are the seeds of its own overcoming.
   This brings up the third, and in the end the largest, meaning of defuturing –
that is, as ‘the kind of conceptual tool necessary to define the unsustainable,
and identify how it takes the future away’ (p. xxix). But as such defuturing
cannot remain descriptive. Since what it describes is threat, it has meaning only
when it becomes also a means to critical action. Defuturing in this third sense
stands for what Elaine Scarry calls those needed, but still absent, ‘appropriate
interpretive categories’ without which we cannot either meaningfully grasp
what is occurring to ourselves and the world or begin to take adequate action
in respect of the crisis we face. In relation to this the first necessity is to bring
the nature of the crises we face to visibility as such, ‘to bring the defutured out
of the shadows’ (p. 126) in order for it to become ‘reviewable’ (p. 51) – in order,
that is, to allow us to think it and to do so without evasion.
  Countering the current rhetoric of ‘the Anthropocene’,6 let alone of the other
terms used to characterize our condition, ‘ecological crisis’, ‘global warming’,
‘climate change’ and so on – all of which in their terminology in effect thrust the
site of the problem back on to nature (as if a more intelligent, understanding
nature, one sympathetic to the economic requirement for expropriation, would
itself take care of these developments) – defuturing insists that this condition
is one that we have created. Defuturing as outcome is our making: the product
of our ‘civilizational model’. Defuturing as a critical concept stands against the
refusal to acknowledge this fact. Against the inability to declare the real problem,
it is the attempt, both rhetorical and analytical, to force an acknowledgement
of the causal structure of this condition – not least on those who wish to
short-circuit real engagement with the depth of the problems, by seeking to
‘solve’ the symptoms of crisis within the parameters of what-is, that is without
moving, mentally or in actuality, from within existing limits – a strategy that Fry
considers impossible. A composite quotation captures the point:

  The basic proposition of defuturing, as a practical philosophy, is that … if
  you do not have a critical recognition of causality … unless you know how
   to read the forms and map the causal picture of the unsustainable in active
   relations of the on-going designing of the defutured, which implicate both
   yourself and your object of engagement, then you are in no position to create
   solutions. Using defuturing to make present that which defutures is the first
   act of informed futuring (p. 239). To fail, or to refuse, to come to this learning
   is to commit oneself to remaining unknowingly within the anthropocentric
  and productivist structures of unsustainability. (p. 126)

Mere assertion or aspiration therefore counts for nothing – and is often
counterproductive. Without a facing up to what-actually-is, no ‘ability to sustain

## Page 14

TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿            xiii

can be brought to hand, no learning of the need for learning otherwise can be
disclosed’ (p. 126). Every evasion of what-is is therefore also an evasion of what-
could-be. If the sustainable is possible, then it must, by definition, be already
immanent in what-is, but it can be won by grasping what-is. ‘What-is’ here
means the history and the trajectory in which we find ourselves. ‘Defuturing’
is an attempt to name this trajectory, both the refusal of the denial of crisis
and the refusal of its too easy depiction. ‘Knowingly offered up as a counter
meta-narrative … with heuristic value’ (p. 54) the aim is both explanatory, to
offer the possibility of understanding, and transformative. As understanding it
is the ‘penetrating beneath’ the appearance of what-is. To penetrate beneath
is to try to elucidate those dynamics of crisis which are not visible, or which
are visible but not ‘seen’ and which, unseen, are incapable of translation into
understanding and action. As transformative it is the aim of using defuturing
as a lever that can ‘turn the acceptedness of omniscient unsustainability into a
foundation to make things otherwise’ (p. 18).
   Defuturing therefore seeks a particular kind of knowledge, a knowledge
that can illuminate the dynamics of the historical development of defuturing
in order, through this, to be able to identify the points at which action can
effectively (and not merely symbolically) intervene. That this knowledge is
required – that we need this knowledge in order to act – is because defuturing/
un-sustainment exists not only as a consequence of economic and technological
imperatives but also, perhaps even centrally, as a consequence of a failure of
understanding.7 ‘We act to defuture’, Fry argues because ‘we have very little
comprehension of the complexity, on-going consequences, and transformative
nature of our impacts’; we do not understand ‘how the values, knowledges,
worlds and things we create go on designing after we have designed and made
them’ (p. 10). What we lack is active knowledge concerning how un-sustainment
is sustained.
   ‘Comprehension’ is a key term here. It does not mean merely passive
understanding, becoming objective about this or that. It means something closer
to the way that Hannah Arendt used this term in the preface to The Origins of
Totalitarianism (1951). Speaking in the wake of the horrors of the first half of
the twentieth century, and addressing the problems she saw intellectuals having
with coming to terms with all that had transpired, Arendt says:

  Comprehension does not mean denying the outrageous, deducing the
   unprecedented from precedents, or explaining phenomena by such analogies
  and generalizations that the impact of reality and the shock of experience are
  no longer felt. It means rather examining and bearing consciously the burden
   our century has placed on us – neither denying its existence nor submitting
  meekly to its weight. Comprehension, in short, means the unpremeditated,
   attentive facing up to, and resisting of reality – whatever it may be.8

To comprehend in the Fry/Arendt sense is therefore to seek to face, but also
to seek to face down, without evasion, what unavoidably and unprecedently
is in its dimensions of implication and consequence. This facing is itself
transformative. A passage in Defuturing that conveys this occurs in the first

## Page 15

xiv         TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿

chapter. Fry is locating the emergence of defuturing in the history of the
anthropocentric:

  The rise and arrival of Eurocentrism authored a particular anthropocentric
  human being who was to become a global sign and agent of the unsustainable.
   Being human, violence, unsustainability and warring are clearly always
   implicated  in one another.  If  this point  is recognized the agenda of
   environmentalism, design, aesthetics, ethics and politics radically changes.
  The most confronting result of such a recognition is that we are ever locked
   into anthropocentrism.

The gloss on the point is significant:

   [But] there is a great deal of difference between being interpellated by the
   anthropocentric drive and confronting it in such a way as to live, by decision
  and by design, in a disposition of accepted responsibility. (This is an ontology
   rather than a condition of enforced consciousness). (p. 37)

The methodological elements visible here are key: in one and the same moment,
the statement of the depth understanding our placement in the unsustainable
and of the how that faced understanding is the beginning of the possibility of
true praxis (‘the disposition of accepted responsibility’). These two elements
comprise the necessary span of defuturing – or as Fry puts it a sentence in
the opening paragraph of the book, grasping ‘the nature and magnitude of the
defutured’ necessarily changes not only how one accounts for the history and
making of the material world, but how we seek to make it. Defuturing as a
‘method’ is a way of seeing and making that delivers a new foundation of design
which radically transforms understanding of what design is and does (p. 1).
  But the temptation to delimit what is being proposed here to design alone
needs to be resisted. What Fry is seeking to do in Defuturing is to reconstruct
a comprehension of the context in which designing takes place. Context is key
here. Against the ideology of autonomy, it is the insistence that in the end it is
this that is generative here – and in several senses.
   – First of all, already evident in what has been said, the historical: the
showing, as Fry puts it in a memorable phrase, ‘that the historical is as much
before us as it is to our rear’ – and hence that the future too is not a ‘void, but a
time and place constituted by directional forces … set in train in the past and
present, and which flow into the future’ (p. xxix). – Second, if we if we cannot
disengage from our history, equally, there is no ‘outside’ of defuturing nor of
the world defutured. Thought as the condition and trajectory of our times, it
addresses itself ontologically to all (if, in its consequences, not to all equally).9
– Third, neither the condition of defuturing, nor how we approach it, is static.
The twist in comprehension that occurs in Fry’s gloss on the anthropocentric
sees being-in-the-world as a condition of active involvement in the constitution
of past and present (and future), where ‘all that we designate as “life” is actively
involved in given and made environments for life itself to be’ (p. 11).
    This breaking with the ‘conditions of enforced consciousness’ leads to
a transformed model of understanding of what-is, towards what Fry calls, a

## Page 16

TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿         xv

relational sense of becoming. It is this comprehension that both stands against
the condition of defuturing, and becomes the possible basis of futuring. It is that
which both puts the deepest structures that are causative, enabling and directive
of defuturing as condition in question and in the same process re-works the
ontology and understanding of the current conditions of finite existence.

   Relationality is at odds with the model of ‘cause and effect’ that has been at
   the very core of the western metaphysical tradition since its inception two
  and half thousand years ago. Relationality is a way of thought that is not
  based on cause-effect relations but on correlative processes and structures.
    [It] provides a means to think modes-of-being in the world as a condition
   of involvement … it speaks to the inseparability of material and symbolic
   exchange’ and the emergence of the constitutive forms and conditions
   of existence ‘out of conjunctural relations between things material and
   immaterial’. (pp. 11–12)

The too-brief sketch of relationality that Fry gives in these pages should not
be taken as definitive (though it should be read with extreme care). But what
it marks, clearly, is the opening of manner in which defuturing as critical
tool becomes generative – as it must – of critically affirmative possibilities.
‘Relationality’ is also indicative of, and it is the pointer towards, that which is
signalled both in the title of the book and its first paragraph: that defuturing,
which changes, dramatically, ‘how one accounts for the history and making of
the material world’ is hence also ‘the generator of a new design philosophy’
(p. 1).

                                        (2)

                             ‘A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY’

That defuturing generates a new design philosophy means that it does so
by necessity. This can be thought of in two ways. It is already apparent that
defuturing, in Fry’s deployment and use of the term, names and refers to two
things simultaneously: a condition in the real – the onset of the loss of certainty
in the future, an event that is the culmination of the paradox of our making
of the world, becoming also a taking-away of the conditions for life – and a
condition of thought, in fact the necessary condition of thought in order to
even begin to deal with this eventuality.
  Two things are being posed here. The first is that defuturing names a new
domain in the Real, a new ontological condition of our history. But the naming
of this condition calls forth the necessity for a new philosophy (meaning a new
mode of thought) in order to bring this event – this change in the metaphysical
structure of the world and of the conditions of existence – to clarity, and to
consciousness. So we have two things: an event in the real (which is revealed
as the very condition of our history) and a necessary event in thought. To
put this slightly differently, defuturing as condition of our times summons an

## Page 17

xvi         TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿

adequate philosophy into being. ‘Adequate’ here means thought that is capable,
through its comprehension of what-is, of taking adequate action vis-à-vis this
condition. It is this totality of thought-action, this circulation of thinking-
making-doing, that Fry labels ‘a new design philosophy’.
  But what Fry poses as a ‘new design philosophy’ is not ‘a new philosophy
of design’. A philosophy of design would mean the bringing of some aspects
of existent design ‘as-is’ under scope of philosophy ‘as-is’. Not only would
the subject-matter of such a philosophy be confined to what already is, to
what is already said to be design (thereby ruling out grasping design’s roles
defuturing as condition), but it could also not in itself posit practice other than
what-is (thereby ruling out understanding defuturing as generative possibility).
Confined, essentially, to judgement, and unable to deliver a new foundation
of design (both as knowledge and as practice), it would be equally unable to
deliver a different foundation of thought. Incapable of engaging either history
or action, consciousness, practice and reflection would remain in precisely the
same places where they began.
  By contrast, when Fry says that defuturing is the generator of a new design
philosophy, he means that it is generative of – that it demands – a new direction
for designing. A ‘new design philosophy’ is therefore a proposition about
design and about thought in the strongest sense. It proposes designing’s place
anew, and it proposes the necessary role of thought anew (now as including,
not as excluding, acting). The new direction for design that it proposes is not a
direction within design as-is; it is a direction beyond where design now is. It is
also a direction beyond where thought now is.
 — — For design, ‘a new design philosophy’ is thus the teasing out of the
      implications of what defuturing forces into thought and action:
      the bringing of these events to some kind of clarification for
      consciousness, and the articulation of what both needs-to-be and
     what could-be. It is the progressive adaption of thinking concerning
      designing to the changing conditions that the actuality, the
     paradoxes and the critical perspectives of defuturing begin to bring
      into view.
 —— For thought, the proposition is less evident but nonetheless present.
      ‘A new design philosophy’ can after all be read two ways: as ‘a new
      design philosophy’, that is, a new philosophy for designing (as acting in
      the world); and as ‘a new design philosophy’, where the emphasis and
      the implication is for a new philosophy per se – a new philosophy of
      existence and of acting in relation to new conditions of existence. This
     new philosophy is a new [design] philosophy because in some manner,
      both defuturing and designing play generative roles in its constitution.
     Both are understood as, for our world, conditions of truth.

Herbert Simon, forty years ago, already argued that ‘the possibility of creating
a science or sciences of design is exactly as great as the possibility of creating
any science of the Artificial. The two possibilities stand and fall together.’ The

## Page 18

TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿          xvii

ground for Simon’s proposition was that in regards to the artificial we are never
concerned with other than ‘how things might be … not with the necessary
but with the contingent’.10 This thesis implied, even then, that any adequate
understanding of designing must include, must in some manner be grounded
upon (better, should be coterminous with) a philosophy of the artificial – to
which after all design owes its phenomenal existence. But the condition of
defuturing, as in effect the condition – literally the product – of a world become
artificial and in this form irredeemably catastrophic in its trajectory ‘ups the
ante’ on Simon’s thesis. This condition demands a new philosophy of existence
and of acting in relation to these conditions. It demands a philosophy (or better,
a new circulation of thought and acting) that is also at once a philosophy or
thought of the artificial and of designing understood as a condition of acting
in these new circumstances. Fry’s ‘new design philosophy’ is a preparation
for such a philosophy. It opens towards, if it does not yet encompass, a new
circulation of thinking and acting, a new philosophy of the artificial. But this
also too implies that the condition of the artificial – which we now experience
as defuturing in a emphatically literal sense – is the ultimate address, not just
of the philosophy of designing but of designing as a mode of action. The role
of designing – now understood also in terms of what it knows or can know
concerning the artificial, and hence the conditions that impel defuturing (which
from this point on is a permanent condition of existence, that can never be
gainsaid, and never not reckoned with) – to understanding and hence to action.

                                        (3)

                              HISTORY

In Defuturing, this sense of another philosophy, a philosophy adequate to
the artificial, is immanent rather than developed: the form it takes is as a
philosophy of history. Defuturing is such because the ontological condition
that defuturing describes and attempts to critically account for is historical.
Methodologically, the book takes the decision that it will seek to articulate
the premise that the ‘processes and consequences of the unsustainable are …
foundationally and actively inscribed in the material and immaterial “nature”
of our worlds’ (p. 10). These are best revealed not on the basis of abstract
reasoning, but by a concretely and historically grounded ontological shift in
how design is viewed, felt, thought, understood, explained and done (p. 5). The
text therefore offers a philosophy of history in two senses: ‘as the term that
names a method of reading the historical nature of unsustainability in process’
(p. 238); and as that which takes an essential critical distance on the actuality
of now. Without the former, the constitution of the present, of what-is, becomes
impossible to grasp; without the latter, the what-is is reduced to the sheer
actuality of now, which means reduced to what the domination of the drive
to accumulate determines. To assert the necessity of, in effect, a philosophy

## Page 19

xviii        TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿

of history, meaning not at all the necessity of blind fate, but its opposite, the
possibility of gaining another purchase on the conditions and trajectory of now,
is to create the necessary space for praxis. This is the sense of one conclusion
that Fry offers when he attempts a summation of defuturing as the process of
reading of the historical nature of unsustainability in process. As such, it stands

   in direct opposition of the thinking of futurists who create the future as a void
   to be filled. Defuturing shows us that what has already been put in place that
   acts as an agency that defutures, it makes clear that whatever we construct
  we do so in the midst of a populated space of structures, objects, rubble
  and wastelands. It deprives us of the illusion that, as we face the future, we
   stand before a condition of infinite choice and are in command of our fate. It
  removes the liberatory fictions of freedom from the past that come with the
   rhetoric of development, change and progress. At the same time, it confronts
   us with the challenge of remaking, of a new ethics, of responsibility and the
   adventure of the unknown. (p. 238)

It is the philosophy of history that therefore grounds engaging with defuturing.
This explains why, although it makes the claim of philosophy, the book as
received does not appear as a philosophy in the usual sense of the term. A
glance at the table of contents shows that it offers something much closer
to what the initial claim indicated – an ‘archaeology’ of that which defutures.
But if the past, for defuturing, is not ‘another country’; if defuturing cannot be
thought of other than through the insistence on understanding past, present
and future as undissolvably connected; if Fry grounds his book not on the basis
of ‘abstract reasoning’, but ‘by reading a range of contemporary and historical
examples against the grain of established history and common sense’ (p. 10), it
is by no means simply ‘history’ (in the given sense) that is evoked. There are a
variety of reasons for this. Together they reveal the complexity and necessity
of history thought as ‘more than’ – that is, more than how, unthinkingly, we
presume it to be.
  The first concerns Fry’s overall project. The fact that ‘the historical is as much
before us as it is to our rear’ means that the question of ‘how the unsustainable
is historically constituted and how it functions’ (p. ix) is a necessary and non-
dispensable moment of understanding, and therefore also of practice. The
earlier statement applies: ‘If you do not have a critical recognition of causality
then you are in no position to work towards true sustainability’ (p. 287). In
the absence of such understanding, even well intentioned action goes astray.
But ‘the problem’ of causality goes deeper. Adorno puts it in these terms: ‘The
past will have been worked through only when the causes of what happened
then have been eliminated. Only because the causes continue to exist does
the captivating spell of the past remain to this day unbroken.’11 Adorno
was speaking of the German past, but the point applies. In the face of the
seriousness of the condition of defuturing, a sufficient engaging with it cannot
address symptoms alone. Contending with defuturing means contending with
the cause, which means contending with the past. Considered as the pure past,
this might seem to be an impossibility. But as Fry takes pains to point out,

## Page 20

TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿         xix

the past is never only such: it manifests in the present and is directive of the
trajectory from present to future. Because of this the past is never only such.
This means that what appears as ‘the eternal pure past which determines us
[as cause] is itself subjected to [the possibility of] retroactive change’. Žižek
usefully reflects further on this point: this means ‘we are thus simultaneously
less free and more free than we think: we are thoroughly passive, determined
by and dependent on the past, but we have the freedom to define the scope of
this determination, that is to (over) determine the past which will determine
us.’ This leads to the possibility of what Žižek calls the very definition of an
authentic act: ‘the retroactivity of a gesture which re-constitutes this past
itself’.12 ‘In our ordinary activity, we effectively just follow the … co-ordinates
of our identity, while an act proper is the paradox of an actual move which
(retroactively) changes the very … “transcendental” co-ordinates of an agents
being’. Such an act changes, or has the potential to change, ‘the actuality of the
world’: ‘While the pure past is the transcendental condition for our acts, our
acts not only create an actual new reality, they also retroactively change this
very condition.’13
  The beginning of engaging with cause is the construction of acts capable of
seizing hold of the past. The third set of reasons that make history indispensable
to seeking change in the world follows directly from this point. Posited and
necessary change is always a measuring of history. The act takes the measure
of the immeasurability of the situation that determines us – and ‘whose’ power,
as a situation, is that it evades and is the absence of measure. It is this ‘invisible
excess’ of the situation, its apparently boundless state, which stymies, or
appears to stymie, action. Conversely, to measure the situation, to show its
historical determination, is to ‘interrupt’ this excess. It is to give the situation,
however excessive, a configuration14 – meaning that it can then be opened to
re-configuration. To quote from a line that is now a cliché, but which has not
thereby lost all of its truth, if we wish to devise courses of action to change
a situation ‘from the existing to the preferred’, then the situation itself must
first be constituted (grasped) in its configuration – and hence in its strategic
potential.15
  For defuturing this necessity is doubled. As Fry points out in his important
discussions of how defuturing  is  historically constituted (pp. 51–8) the
necessity is both to offer an understanding of history – in the sense of how
defuturing emerges as a condition within the modern developmental process
(as a series of contradictions that gradually become ‘impossible’ to steer within
the limits of capitalism) (p. 238) – and as a counter-historical narrative to the
metanarrative that seeks to explain away (or more simply deny) what in fact
‘is’ as our condition. Philosophically speaking, defuturing is then two things:
a proffered understanding – a grasping – of history, and a ‘constructed meta-
object’, which re-reads this history, grasps its configuration – and its strategic
potential – differently. This is history not as simply offered as ‘the past’, but,
as Fry puts it, as that which an ‘bring what has to be learnt into being, as an
obligation to find a way to act’ (p. 54).

## Page 21

xx         TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿

  The fourth set of factors that  in Defuturing necessitate history are
circumstantial – though not to be disparaged on this basis. Not only was 1998
in many ways too early to broach the philosophical depth that Fry’s work
was pointing to – not least because of the all but impossibility of publishing
such work in book form at the time (Defuturing had a difficult time getting
published as it was: the unfamiliarity of the concepts, let alone the proposition
that design might necessitate and deserve serious thought was, to put  it
mildly, a difficult proposition to advance to publishers twenty years ago) – but
‘circumstantial’ also applies here in a more tactical sense. As the preface tells,
the book had its genesis in a series of public lectures given in the Faculty of
Design at the University of Technology in Sydney. The lectures had to develop
therefore, in large part, from where the audience was situated. Fry understood
this from the beginning. To ‘try to speak a new language’ (p. 11), to open ‘an
entirely new way of understanding the worlds humans have made, and what
humans do’ (p. 18), cannot begin sui generis. It has to begin by denaturalizing
and defamiliarizing what-is. This is why, as Fry puts it, ‘so much of what is
being attempted here goes to the ambition of de-familiarizing the familiar in
order for it to become reviewable’ (p. 60), that is, capable of being thought.
The problem is that the familiar assures us all too easily that what-is ‘is’. In
regard to the past, it assures us that it leads directly to what-is and thus need
not be thought about. In regard to the future, it makes the latter thinkable
only as extrapolation from what-is (and therefore allows us to think change
only within given limits, these limits disguised as ‘innovation’ or the New). By
contrast, if defuturing is learning how to acquire the agency, ‘to destroy one
designing, making and dwelling, in order to create something other (sustain-
ability)’ (p. 51), it has to begin by defamiliarizing what is familiar and its given
histories. This is why, being on design, the lectures, and thus book begin from
design, that is, from that which is in some ways all too familiar, the history of
designing in the twentieth century.
  What matters however in this process – what actually makes the familiar
de-familiar, what makes the familiar open to reflection – is the attitude taken
to this material; the manner in which it seeks not to repeat the patterns ‘of
established history and assumed common sense’. The requirement (and this
touches on design historical method in general) is brush history ‘against
the grain’ (p. 10); to use the familiar differently, to make it do other work.
This is a use of history and a use of design to enable both to be thought
otherwise:

   While design history is employed by this text, what is written here is not
   itself design history and is not intended to add to this body of knowledge.
  What it does though is to use historical material on design to expose the
   archaeology of another ground, the ground of the negation of the future –
   defuturing. The history of what has been made present by design is employed
   parasitically to expose what design has taken away. History is thus employed
   against history, and out of this recognition the entire frame of reference of
   design shifts. (p. 2)

## Page 22

TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿         xxi

In this passage, neither design nor history remains conceptually intact.
Design, because the apparent given of its ground shifts, and the perspective
changes. When Fry says that in this book the ‘narratives of history, especially
design history and histories of modernity and technology, are being used
… in order to say something through rather than about’ design, something
methodologically crucial is being asserted. The address of design shifts. It is
no longer itself, it is history. Or better still, the history of the artificial and of
the patterns of existence we construct in relation to what the artificial opens
and closes for us.
   This in turn places ‘design history’ in question. The latter deploys (usually
social or cultural, sometimes technological, but rarely economic) history in
the service of giving context to a quasi-autonomous process or institution,
the construction of whose autonomy is rarely placed in question. But as
sub-history that employs, but does not itself contribute to history, design
history in its received modes cannot, except in the most minor of ways, throw
light on history. About the conditions that secure what-is (and which have
constructed and secured in place ‘Design’), it has structurally nothing to say.
In respect of defuturing, even such a thing as the concealment of the crisis of
unsustainability largely eludes it.16
   Finally, in this process, history (‘the co-ordinates of our activity’) itself
shifts. The reason why design deployed as lens is capable of producing insight
is that by necessity in its activity (even in its normal professional activity)
design deals, endemically, with the negotiation of incommensurable relations,
above all those of nature and artifice, subject and object.17 Design, in other
words, does not simply replicate the determining binary oppositions of
ideology, on the contrary, understood in terms of what it achieves design is
that which, poised between dominant divisions, makes ‘binary oppositions
untenable’ (p. 241).
  What this means is that as lens on history, design is double. It is at once a
lens onto its own role in un-sustainment and on un-sustainment in general. But
because it also, pushes beyond the binary oppositions that are in part determinant
of un-sustainment (the binary oppositions on which the modern depends), so
design also offers, in effect, a redemptive perspective on what-is. It throws into
view the ‘one-sidedness’ of what-is, and thus shows, in part, what should-be.
If ‘sustainability demands a changed foundation of thought and value with
which to define problems and on which to build and judge … actions’ (p. 240),
one part of that foundation is gained by being able to create an understanding
of (modern) historical development that can see the contradictions of the
movements of that history and that which, within this history, points, even in
subterranean ways, to what already hints at or embodies other ways of acting.
To make again the obvious point. If sustainability (or a beyond to defuturing as
condition) is possible, this is because that beyond is already incipient in some
manner in what-is (sustainment, or futuring, cannot come from without: no God
can descend – this is precisely the meaning of Nietzsche’s ‘God is Dead’).

## Page 23

xxii        TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿

                                         (4)

                   THE STRUCTURE OF THE BOOK

It now becomes possible to see the structure of the book. Fry offers useful
paraphrases of the whole and its essential parts (see pp. 13–14, 19–20, 51–7
and 145–6).
   In terms of defuturing Fry develops a threefold structure of attention of
attention, as at once a condition (the inescapable condition of our times) as
a critical tool (that through which we enabled to grasp – to comprehend –
our condition) and (via reflection on what constitutes defuturing) the basis on
which we can begin to think of other relations to nature and our practice, that
is precisely by thinking relationally.
   This ‘structure of attention’ is deployed however in the context of lectures
given (as noted) to a largely undergraduate audience and which are themselves
grounded on history. Here too there is a threefold structure. The book begins
with the conceptual and historical grounding of defuturing and of method
(Chapter 1), moves through ‘problems of history’ in the history of modernity
in design as ‘they impinge upon address[ing] the relations between defuturing,
Design and the grand narratives of design history’ (Chapters 2–4) which are
then questioned, turned and expanded in other ways in a series of thematic
studies (Chapters 5–8) that ‘undercut’ both the sufficiency of history and the
conceptualizations of design and technology that we been reliant upon across
the modern period.
   Together these three moments work to reveal the all but absolute inadequacy
of the given view of ‘Design’. Inadequate, that is, not only in its conceptualization
of the practices of design, but also especially in relation to the complexity of
the instantiation of defuturing. Design is shown here as both requiring ‘major
questions of sustainability’ to be put to it and as ‘far more complex [in its work]
than our descriptive language is able to articulate’ (p. 14). These themes of the
necessity of the question and of the adequacy of descriptive language to the
phenomena we are dealing with, carry through the book as a whole. In one
sense, they repeat what is known. In another, they do not.
   In the moment of its first publication two decades ago Defuturing presented
a radically new understanding of the history, context and futures of designing.
It offered, then as now, a prescient account of what it means to comprehend
that we live in world that is taking away futures for ourselves and non-human
others. It is tempting, inevitably, to presume that time erases its force as thought
‘catches up’ to argument – not least because Fry’s own work across the last two
decades in some ways goes past the point where this text ended. Yet in a second
sense, while limitations of the book are easily seen, less perceived perhaps and
certainly less critically articulated, is the double implication of designing that
Fry manages here: first, in its roles in helping to create the unsustainable, but
second, re-thought through the lens of defuturing, as a mode of acting in the
world that can help contest the negation of the world. The critical challenge
for readers of Defuturing remains the attempt to advance our comprehension
of designing and of how futures can be constituted: historically, through

## Page 24

TONY FRY’S DEFUTURING: A NEW DESIGN PHILOSOPHY﻿          xxiii

exploration of the (complex) and dialectical archaeology of defuturing and
of designing’s complex relation to un-sustainment; and analytically, through
the challenge of articulating the roles of designing in modes of new practical
reasoning and ways of acting in the world.
